Defining the Generation (Qarn) and Its Durations
Defines qarn and gives durations of early generations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of qarn (generation) and the specific years associated with the first three generations of Muslims.

Core Idea
A generation is defined by shared characteristics and time, not a fixed number of years.

This lesson unpacks the author's technical definition of 'qarn' (generation), a term often misunderstood as simply 100 years. The passage provides both the linguistic rationale and the precise timelines for the Companions, Followers, and Followers of the Followers.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of qarn
Start with the passage: 'Wal-qarn: ahlu zamanin wahidin mutaqarib...' Understand the definition and the reason it is called qarn.
Notice the linguistic root: qarn from qarana (to join).
2
Examine the three generations
The passage lists three generations: the Companions, the Followers, and the Followers of the Followers. Note the specific years given for each.
The Companions' generation is 120 years, the Followers from 100 AH to about 170 AH, and the next to about 220 AH.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why these specific generations and timelines matter. They highlight the continuity and transmission of knowledge from the Prophet's era onward.
This is important for understanding the chain of narration in hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم النبوات
وخيرهم مَن وُلِّيَ الخلافة
والقَرْن: أهل زمانٍ واحدٍ مُتقارب، اشتركوا في أمرٍ من الأمور المقصودة. وسُمِّي قرنا : لأنَّه يَقْرِنُ أُمَّةً بأُمَّةٍ وعالما بعالم ، ثم جُعل اسما للوقت أو لأهله ، فقرنه الا الله مُدَّة أصحابه من البعث إلى آخر من مات منهم ، وهي مائة وعشرون سنة ، أو نفس أصحابه ، وقرن التابعينَ: مِن سنة مائة إلى نحو سبعين ، وقرن أتباع التابعين : تمَّ إلى حدود العشرين ومائتين، والله أعلم
وقوله: (فَاسْتَمِعْ) ، تكملةٌ
Section of prophethoods. And the best of them is the one who was entrusted with the caliphate. And al-qarn (the generation): people of one close time period, who share in some of the intended matters. And it was called a 'qarn' because it connects a nation to a nation and a world to a world. Then it was made a name for the time or its people. So the qarn of the Companions of Allah is the duration of his Companions from the mission to the last of them who died, which is one hundred and twenty years, or it is the Companions themselves. And the qarn of the Followers: from year one hundred to about seventy. And the qarn of the Followers of the Followers: completed to about the two hundred and twenties. And Allah knows best. And His saying: 'So listen' is a completion.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage is both a doctrinal statement and a lexical definition. It begins by stating that the best of the Companions is the one who was given the caliphate, referring to the Rightly Guided Caliphs. Then it defines the term 'qarn' (generation) as a group of people from a close time period who share common characteristics. The reason it is called 'qarn' is because it connects one nation and world to another. Over time, it became used for either the time period or the people of that time. It then specifies the durations: the generation of the Companions spans from the Prophet's mission until the death of the last Companion, about 120 years. Alternatively, it may refer to the Companions themselves. The generation of the Followers is from around year 100 AH to about 170 AH, and the generation of the Followers of the Followers extends to around 220 AH. The passage ends with a note that the word 'fastami' in the poem is a completion.

Key Terms
قرن (qarn/generation)
تابعون (Followers)
أتباع التابعين (Followers of the Followers)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A generation (qarn) is always exactly 100 years.
Correction: The passage defines qarn by shared characteristics and gives specific durations that are not necessarily 100 years, e.g., the Companions are 120 years.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how long was the generation of the Companions?
A) 100 years
B) 120 years
C) 70 years
D) Until the Prophet's death

Key Takeaway
A generation is defined by its people and shared characteristics, with the Companions spanning 120 years, the Followers from 100–170 AH, and the next up to 220 AH.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Companions' generation lasted 120 years. Do you see them as distant historical figures or as living examples whose teachings are immediately relevant to your life? What specific action will you take today to draw closer to their legacy?
?
The Followers lived from 100 AH to 170 AH—closer to our time. Does knowing the precise lengths of these generations make you feel part of an unbroken chain? What are you doing to strengthen your link in that chain for the generations after you?
?
The text defines a generation by shared characteristics, not just time. What shared characteristics of faith and practice do you have with the early Muslims? Name one quality they possessed that you lack, and commit to a concrete step to develop it this week.

Definition of the Tabi'i (Follower) and Conditions
Defines a Tabi'i and discusses conditions for being considered a Follower

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of a Tabi'i and the conditions required for someone to be considered a Follower, as well as the differing scholarly views on the necessity of companionship.

Core Idea
A Tabi'i is defined by meeting a Companion who met the Prophet, with conditions on life and faith.

This lesson establishes the definition of the Tabi'i, the second generation of Muslims after the Companions. The source passage presents the primary definition and a differing view, highlighting the importance of meeting conditions. Understanding these distinctions helps in evaluating hadith narrations and recognizing the status of the Followers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice that the passage ends abruptly, possibly indicating a continuation.
2
Identify the two definitions of Tabi'i
The passage presents a primary definition and then a second opinion. Identify the key elements of each: the first requires meeting a Companion who met the Prophet, both alive and believing, in a normal meeting; the second adds the condition of companionship.
Also note that 'discernment' (tamyiz) is not required for a Tabi'i.
3
Reflect on the conditions and their implications
Consider why these conditions matter for the classification of narrators and the transmission of hadith. How does the definition affect the reliability of a Tabi'i as a narrator?
The condition of 'normal meeting' excludes extraordinary meetings like meeting a dead Companion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَتَابِعِيْ يعني أَنَّ رُتبتَهم تَلِي رتبة الصَّحابة مِن غير تراخ كبير. والتّابعيُّ: مَن لَقي الصَّحابي الَّذِي لَقِيه صلى الله عليه وسلم ، حيًّا مؤمنًا حيَّا مؤمنا به، لقيا على غير وجه حرق العادة. وقيل: لا يكفي مجرَّد اللقاء ، بل لا بُدَّ مِن الصحبة لمزيَّة لقائه على لقاء غيره من صلحاء أمَّتِه ، ولا يشترط فيه التمييز ، ولو شُرِط في الصَّحابي
Then the Followers: meaning that their rank follows the rank of the Companions without great delay. And the Tabi'i (Follower) is: one who met the Companion who met him (peace be upon him), alive and believing, alive and believing in him, a meeting not in an extraordinary manner. And it is said: mere meeting is not sufficient, rather companionship is necessary due to the superiority of his meeting over meeting others among the righteous of his community. And discernment (tamyiz) is not required of him, even if it is required of the Companion.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'Tabi'i' (Follower), the second generation of Muslims after the Companions. It first explains that the rank of the Followers follows that of the Companions without a long gap. Then it defines a Tabi'i as one who met a Companion who himself met the Prophet, while both were alive and believing, in a normal (non-miraculous) meeting. It then presents a differing view that mere meeting is not enough; rather, there must be some form of companionship to distinguish his meeting from that of other righteous people. Finally, it states that discernment (tamyiz, i.e., the ability to distinguish) is not required for a Tabi'i, even if it is required for a Companion (which is discussed elsewhere). The passage is cut off before completing the comparison.

Key Terms
تابعي (Tabi'i/Follower)
صحابي (Companion)
لقيا (meeting)
تمييز (discernment)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that simply meeting any Companion automatically qualifies one as a Tabi'i.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the Companion met must have met the Prophet, both must be alive and believing, and the meeting must be normal. Additionally, some scholars require actual companionship beyond just meeting.

Checkpoint
What is the minimum condition for being a Tabi'i according to the first definition?
A) To have accompanied a Companion for a long time
B) To have met a Companion who met the Prophet, both alive and believing
C) To have seen the Prophet
D) To be a righteous Muslim

Key Takeaway
A Tabi'i is primarily defined as one who met a Companion who met the Prophet, alive and believing, in a normal meeting, with differing views on the necessity of companionship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you truly grateful for the chain of Companions and Followers who transmitted the faith to you? What is one concrete way you can honor their sacrifice in your daily life starting today?
?
The lesson distinguishes between mere 'meeting' and 'companionship' with the Prophet's legacy. Do you merely 'meet' the Quran and Sunnah in passing, or do you develop companionship with them? What would change if you sought deeper companionship this week?
?
You are alive and believing right now, just like the Followers were when they met the Companions. But are you spiritually alive? If your faith feels stagnant, what one step will you take before tonight to revive it?

The Six Companions Completing the Ten
Al-Laqani defines the six companions after the four caliphs and their rank.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand who the six companions completing the ten promised Paradise are and the principle that no explicit text specifies superiority among them.

Core Idea
The six companions are known but their ranking among themselves is unknown without divine text.

This lesson introduces the six companions who complete the ten promised Paradise. Al-Laqani defines them as following the four caliphs in rank, but emphasizes that no revelation distinguishes one over another within the six. You will learn their names and the key principle of relying on divine instruction in matters of virtue.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and commentary
Read the Arabic verse and its translation. Then read the commentary carefully to understand each part.
The commentary defines each term in the verse.
2
Identify the six companions
From the commentary, list the six companions who complete the ten promised Paradise.
They are: Talha, al-Zubayr, Abd al-Rahman, Sa'd, Sa'id, and Abu Ubayda.
3
Understand the rule of no text
Notice the statement 'لم يرد نص بتفاوت بعضهم على بعض' – no text came distinguishing them, so no one asserts superiority. This is a key principle in Ahl al-Sunnah: ranking among companions requires textual evidence.
4
Note the exception for kinship and precedence
The commentary adds that this disregard for superiority is only when not considering kinship, precedence in Islam, or emigration. Otherwise, those with such merits are better from that aspect.
This shows that superiority is multifaceted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يَلِيهِمُ قَومٌ كِرَامٌ بَرَرَهُ عِدَّتُهُم سِتٌ تمامُ العَشَرة
(يَلِيهِمُ )، أي: يلي آخر الأربعةِ الخُلفَاءِ في الأفضلية على الغَيْرِ (قَوْمُ)، أي: رِجال (كِرَامٌ) - جمع كريم جمع كريم ، وهو كريم النَّفس رفيع النَّسب (بَرَرَهُ) جمع بَر ، وهو المُحْسن عِدَّتُهُمْ سِتَّ)، أي: ستَّةٌ (تَمَامُ العَشَرَة) المبشَّرينَ بالجنَّة، الذين من جملتهم المشايخ الأربعة السابقون، وهم: طلحة بن عبيد الله ، والزبير بن العوام ابن عمَّة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ، وعبد الرحمن بن عوف، وسعد بن أبي وقاص ، وسعيد بن زيد ، وأبو عبيدة بن الجراح، ولم يرد نص بتفاوت بعضهم على بعض في الأفضلية فلا قائل به ؛ لعدم التوقيف وتخصيص هؤلاء العشرة؛ لِشُهرة حديثهم الجامع لهم(١) ، وإن كان المُبشَّرون بالجَنَّة أكثر (٢) .
ثمَّ هذا مَعَ قَطْع النَّظرِ عن القرابة الشَّريفة(۳)، والتَّقدُّم في الإسلام والهجرة (٤) ، بدليل قوله آنفًا: «والسابقون فضلهم ؛ نصا عرف .
(١) قوله: (الجامع لهم أي الجامع لذكرهم في مجلس واحد. الشنواني.
(٢) قوله : ( وإن كان المبشرون بالجنة أكثر ) فإن الحسن والحسين مبشران بالجنة ، وأمهما كذلك. الشنواني .
(۳) قوله: (هذا مع قطع النظر عن القرابة ...) أي: عدم تفضيل هؤلاء الستة بعضهم على بعض إنما هو إذا قطعنا النظر عن كون أحدهم له قرابة بالنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ، أو كونه سابقا في الإسلام، أو الهجرة، وإلا فيقال بتفضيله من هذه الجهة ، فالقريب له أفضل، والمتقدم من هؤلاء أفضل من غيره، والسابق في الهجرة منهم أفضل من متأخرها . الشنواني .
(٤) قال العلامة الدهلوي الله في عقيدته: «ولا نعني الأفضلية من جميع الوجوه، حتى يَعُمَّ=
They are followed by a noble righteous people, their number is six, completing the ten. (They are followed) i.e. they follow the last of the four caliphs in superiority over others; (a people) i.e. men; (noble) plural of karim, and he is noble of soul and high of lineage; (righteous) plural of barr, and he is the benefactor; (their number is six) i.e. six; (completing the ten) promised Paradise, among whom are the previous four shaykhs, and they are: Talha ibn Ubaydullah, al-Zubayr ibn al-Awwam the cousin of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf, Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, Sa'id ibn Zayd, and Abu Ubayda ibn al-Jarrah. No text has come distinguishing some of them over others in superiority, so no one asserts it due to lack of divine instruction (tawqif). The specification of these ten is due to the fame of the hadith that gathers them, even though those promised Paradise are more. Then this is with disregard for noble kinship, precedence in Islam, and emigration, as evidence of his earlier statement: 'And the predecessors, their virtue is known by text.' (1) His statement: 'the hadith that gathers them' means the hadith that mentions them in one gathering. Al-Shanwani. (2) His statement: 'even though those promised Paradise are more' for al-Hasan and al-Husayn are promised Paradise, and their mother likewise. Al-Shanwani. (3) His statement: 'this with disregard for kinship...' i.e. the lack of preference of these six over each other is only when we disregard one of them having kinship with the Prophet, or being earlier in Islam or emigration; otherwise, preference is said from this aspect, so the relative has more virtue, and the earlier among them is better than another, and the earlier in emigration among them is better than the later. Al-Shanwani. (4) The scholar al-Dihlawi said in his creed: 'We do not mean superiority in all aspects, so that it encompasses...'
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage is a verse from the poem followed by its commentary. It defines the six companions who follow the four caliphs in rank, completing the ten promised Paradise. The commentary explains each term: 'they are followed' means they come after the four caliphs in superiority; 'people' means men; 'noble' means generous of soul and high lineage; 'righteous' means benefactors; 'their number is six' refers to the six remaining of the ten. It then names the six: Talha, al-Zubayr, Abd al-Rahman, Sa'd, Sa'id, and Abu Ubayda. The commentary emphasizes that no explicit text (nass) specifies an order of superiority among these six, so no one claims such due to the lack of divine instruction (tawqif). The ten are specified because of the famous hadith that gathers them together, though there are more than ten promised Paradise, like al-Hasan and al-Husayn. The passage then notes that this ranking disregards considerations of kinship, precedence in Islam, and emigration; otherwise, those with such merits would be superior from that aspect. The commentary also quotes al-Dihlawi's creed about the meaning of superiority not being comprehensive of all virtues.

Key Terms
الصحابة
العشرة المبشرون بالجنة
الأفضلية
التوقيف
الخلفاء الأربعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the ten promised Paradise are the only ones who are guaranteed Paradise.
Correction: The passage states that those promised Paradise are more than ten, mentioning al-Hasan and al-Husayn as examples.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is there no assertion of superiority among the six companions listed?
A) Because they were all equal in all aspects
B) Because no explicit text (nass) from revelation distinguishes between them
C) Because they were all from the Muhajirun
D) Because the Prophet forbade ranking them

Key Takeaway
The six companions after the four caliphs are known, but their relative merit is not specified by revelation, so no one asserts superiority among them without evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the Muslims around you, do you allow yourself to rank them in your heart without clear evidence from the Quran and Sunnah? How does this lesson's emphasis on refraining from such judgment (due to lack of divine text) challenge your tendency to judge others based on your own perceptions?
?
The companions themselves did not have a known ranking among them; if they refrained from asserting superiority, how much more should you avoid feeling superior to other Muslims based on your own deeds? In what specific situation today can you actively lower yourself and honor another Muslim simply because you do not know their standing with Allah?
?
This lesson reminds us that only Allah's text can determine true superiority. Are there areas in your life where you base your self-worth or the worth of others on worldly measures (wealth, lineage, etc.) instead of the unseen scales of taqwa? What is one concrete action you can take today to treat someone as potentially more virtuous than yourself?

The Rank and Number of the People of Badr
Al-Laqani states the rank of the people of Badr after the six companions and gives their number.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rank of the people of Badr among the companions, their number, and the correct interpretation of the hadith of forgiveness.

Core Idea
The people of Badr rank just below the six foremost companions, and their forgiveness is assured.

This passage establishes the high status of the participants of the Battle of Badr. Al-Laqani places them immediately after the six companions among the ten promised Paradise, and provides their exact numbers. He also addresses the famous hadith in which Allah tells them they may do as they please, explaining its true meaning to prevent misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic verse and its commentary, then the translation. Notice that the verse mentions both the people of Uhud and the Pledge of Ridwan, but the commentary focuses on Badr.
The phrase 'قَبَيْعَةُ' is likely a typo for 'وبيعة' (and the Pledge).
2
Identify the rank of the people of Badr
Find the sentence that states their rank relative to the six among the ten (the 'ashara mubashshara). The commentary says: 'رتبتهم تلي رتبة السَّتَّة من العشرة'.
3
Note the numbers of participants
Count the numbers given: 317 men from humans, 70 from jinn, and 3000 from angels. Understand that the number 317 is considered correct, with dispute about additional up to 70.
4
Examine the hadith and its interpretation
Read the hadith 'Do what you will, for I have forgiven you' and the commentary that explains it is not permission to sin but an honor. Then consider the example of Qudama ibn Maz'un who was punished for drinking wine despite being a Badri.
This shows the hadith does not mean they can sin without consequence.
5
Understand the correct interpretation
The commentary clarifies that the hadith means Allah will forgive them or grant them repentance, not that they are free to sin. The case of Qudama shows they are still subject to Divine law.
This guards against the misconception that the people of Badr are above the law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأهلُ أُحُدْ قَبَيْعَةُ الرّضْوانِ ، فأهلُ بَدْرٍ العَظِيمِ الشَّانِ
(فَأَهْلُ) غزوة ( بَدْرٍ) رتبتهم تلي رتبة السَّتَّة من العشرة ، سواء استُشْهِدوا فيها أو لا(١) ، ـ وبدر : اسمٌ للوادي ، أو لبئر فيه ـ وكانوا ثلاثمائة وسبعة عشر (۲) رجلًا مِن الإنس، قيل: وسبعون مِن الجِنِّ، وثلاثة آلاف من الملائكة .
(١) قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في أهل بدر [وهو عند البخاري ومسلم]: «اطلع الله على أهل بدر فقال : اعملوا ما شئتم فقد غفرتُ لكم وليسَ المُراد ظاهرُ اللفظ مِن الإباحة ؛ فإنه خلاف عقد الشرع ، بل تشريفهم وتكريمهم بعدم المؤاخذة، أو يوفقوا للتوبة، وقيل: هي شهادة بعدم وقوع الذنب، قال الشامي: وفيه نظر ظاهرُ ؛ فقدامة بن مظعون شرب الخمر في أيام عمر وكان بدريا. الأمير. وكان شرب الخمر متأولا قوله تعالى: لَيْسَ عَلَى الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا الصَّالِحَتِ جُنَاحٌ فِيمَا طَعِمُوا إِذَا مَا اتَّقَواْ وَءَامَنُواْ وَعَمِلُوا الصَّلِحَتِ ثُمَّ اتَّقَواْ وَءَامَنُوا ثُمَّ اتَّقَوا وَأَحْسَنُوا وَاللَّهُ يُحِبُّ الْمُحْسِنِينَ ﴾ [المائدة: ۹۳ ] فحدّه عمر ، وعزله عن البحرين، وتوفي سنة (٣٦هـ) قال أبو أيوب السختياني: (لم يُحَدَّ في الخمر بدري سواه) لكن استدرك عليه الذهبي وقال: بلى ونعيمان بن عمرو الأنصاري صاحب المزاح).
(۲) قوله: ( وسبعة عشر ) أي : على الصحيح ، لأنه اختلف في الزائد إلى السبعين ، وفي السيرة الشامية : أنه أمر بعدهم؛ فأُخبر بأنهم ثلاثمئة وثلاثة عشر؛ ففرح بذلك، وقال: عدة أصحاب جالوت . الشنواني .
So the people of Uhud and the Pledge of Ridwan, then the people of Badr of great status. (So the people) of the battle of (Badr), their rank follows the rank of the six among the ten, whether they were martyred therein or not. And Badr is a name of the valley, or of a well therein. And they were three hundred and seventeen men from humans, and it is said seventy from jinn, and three thousand from angels. (1) The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said concerning the people of Badr – and it is with al-Bukhari and Muslim: 'Allah looked upon the people of Badr and said: Do what you will, for I have forgiven you.' And the apparent meaning of the wording is not intended as permission, for that contradicts the contract of the Law; rather it is honoring and ennobling them by not holding them accountable, or that they will be granted repentance. And it is said it is a testimony that no sin occurs from them. Al-Shami said: There is an obvious problem in this; for Qudama ibn Maz'un drank wine in the days of Umar, and he was a Badri. Al-Amir. And he drank wine interpreting the words of Allah the Exalted: 'There is no blame upon those who have believed and done righteous deeds concerning what they have eaten...' (al-Ma'idah 93). So Umar imposed the prescribed punishment on him and dismissed him from Bahrain, and he died in 36 AH. Abu Ayyub al-Sakhtiyani said: 'No Badri was punished for wine except him.' But al-Dhahabi corrected him and said: Yes, and Nu'ayman ibn Amr al-Ansari, the jester. (2) His statement: 'seventeen' i.e. according to the correct view, for there is disagreement concerning the extra up to seventy. And in al-Sira al-Shamiyya: that he sent after them, and was informed that they were three hundred and thirteen, so he rejoiced at that and said: The number of the companions of Goliath. Al-Shanwani.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage is a verse and its commentary on the ranking of the companions after the six. The verse mentions the people of Uhud and the Pledge of Ridwan, then the people of Badr. However, the commentary focuses only on the people of Badr, explaining that their rank follows that of the six among the ten promised Paradise, regardless of whether they were martyred at Badr or not. It notes that Badr is the name of the valley or a well there. The number of human participants was 317, with a reported 70 jinn and 3000 angels. The commentary then includes a lengthy footnote discussing the hadith of forgiveness for the people of Badr, explaining that the apparent meaning is not permissiveness to sin, but rather honor and assurance of forgiveness or repentance. It addresses the case of Qudama ibn Maz'un, a Badri who was punished for drinking wine, and notes that he misinterpreted the verse in al-Ma'idah. The footnote also mentions that only one Badri (or two) were punished for wine. The second footnote clarifies that the number 317 is the correct one, as there is dispute about additional numbers up to 70, and cites the story of the Prophet rejoicing at the number matching the companions of Goliath.

Key Terms
أهل بدر
الستة من العشرة
الغزوة
الملائكة
الجن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith 'Do what you will, for I have forgiven you' means the people of Badr can sin without punishment.
Correction: The commentary states the apparent meaning is not intended; it is honoring them and does not abrogate the Sharia. The example of Qudama being punished for wine confirms that Badris are still accountable.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the rank of the people of Badr?
A) They are the highest rank among all companions
B) Their rank follows the rank of the six among the ten promised Paradise
C) Their rank is equal to the four caliphs
D) They are ranked below the people of Uhud

Key Takeaway
The people of Badr hold a distinguished rank second only to the six foremost companions, and the hadith of forgiveness is an honor, not a license to sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes think that because of your good deeds or status, you are immune from sin? How does the story of Qudama ibn Maz'un challenge that assumption, and what does it demand of you today?
?
The Prophet (peace be upon him) said 'Do what you will, for I have forgiven you' to the people of Badr, yet scholars clarified it is not a license. Are there areas in your life where you have misinterpreted Allah's mercy as permission to be careless? What will you change after learning this?
?
If the people of Badr, despite their lofty rank, were not free from accountability, what does that mean for your own sense of security in your faith? How will you balance hope in Allah's forgiveness with the fear of His justice, starting now?

Ranking of the people of Badr, Uhud, and the Pledge of Ridwan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ranking of the Companions based on their participation in the events of Badr, Uhud, and the Pledge of Ridwan, and the reasoning behind this hierarchy.

Core Idea
Participation in Badr, Uhud, and the Pledge of Ridwan establishes a clear hierarchy among the Companions.

This lesson explores how the commentator ranks the Companions according to their involvement in three pivotal events: the Battle of Badr, the Battle of Uhud, and the Pledge of Ridwan. The ranking reflects both spiritual merit and the special favor of Allah. We will also learn the story behind the Pledge of Ridwan.

The ranking is: Badr > Uhud > Pledge of Ridwan.

Section of prophethoods. They attended it (1).. He refutes what preceded from that the rank of angels follows the rank of prophets, and among the angels those who witnessed Badr are better than those who did not witness it, the superiority; yes, and by analogy it is said likewise for believing jinn. And he restricted by the description of Badr, which is: 'the great significance', from its other two expeditions, for its expeditions are three (2), the greatest of them is the middle one, due to the presence of angels and jinn in it with humans. (So the people of) the expedition of (Uhud) (3) - a mountain known in Medina (4) -, their rank follows the rank of the rest of the people of Badr (5), and the intended meaning: those who witnessed it from the Muslims, whether martyred in it like the seventy or not, and its people were one thousand three hundred of the hypocrites who turned back with the people of Abdullah ibn Ubayy. Determination of the foremost from the Companions: (The Pledge), meaning: the rank of the people of the Pledge of (Ridwan) follows the rank of the people of Uhud, and it was called the Pledge of Ridwan (1) due to His statement, the Exalted: 'Certainly Allah was pleased with the believers' [al-Fath: 18]. And they were one thousand four hundred, and it was said: and five hundred; the Prophet set out with them to visit the House. The polytheists prevented him; so he sent Uthman to them for peace, and it spread that they had killed him; so he said at that: 'We will not leave until we engage them in war' and he called the people at the tree for the pledge upon death, or that they would not flee, and they pledged allegiance to him upon that, and none abstained from it except Ibn Qays, who was a hypocrite, hidden under the belly of his camel, and he was the cousin of al-Bara' ibn Ma'rur, and he was also of those whose hearts are to be reconciled, and it is said that he repented and his Islam became good. Then the life of Uthman became clear, and the Prophet made a treaty with them on a condition and returned to Medina.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage is a ranking of the Companions according to virtue, based on historical events. The commentator begins by addressing a previous point about angels: the rank of angels is after prophets, but among angels, those who witnessed Badr are superior to those who did not. He extends this reasoning to believing jinn. He then specifies that the description 'the great significance' for Badr distinguishes it from its other two expeditions (the first and third), because the middle one (Badr) involved angels and jinn. Next, he states that the people of Uhud come after the rest of the people of Badr. 'People of Uhud' refers to all Muslims who were present, whether martyred or not. He notes that one thousand three hundred hypocrites turned back at Uhud. Under the subheading 'Determination of the foremost from the Companions,' he discusses the Pledge of Ridwan: its rank follows that of Uhud. It is named after the verse in which Allah declares His pleasure. He narrates the story: the Prophet set out with 1400 or 1500 to visit the Kaaba; the polytheists blocked them; Uthman was sent and rumored killed; the Prophet called for a pledge under a tree to fight to the death; all except a hypocrite named Ibn Qays pledged; later Uthman was found alive, and the Prophet made a treaty and returned. This story illustrates the virtue of those who made the pledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Companions earned ranks through presence in moments of extreme trial—Badr (facing a larger army), Uhud (facing defeat), and the Pledge (willingness to die). ?
This lesson shows that even among the righteous there is a hierarchy based on action, not just belief. If your faith were evaluated by your willingness to be present when it costs you something, where would you stand? What is one comfort or safety you are clinging to that you need to abandon for Allah's pleasure?
?
The hypocrite Ibn Qays hid from the Pledge of Ridwan. Are there areas in your life where you are 'hiding' from a commitment that demands your full presence—whether in prayer, charity, or standing for truth? Ask Allah to give you the courage to come out from under the belly of the camel.

Definition of the Foremost Muslims
Definition of the foremost Muslims as those who prayed to both qiblas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the foremost Muslims are defined according to Abu Musa al-Ash'ari and other scholars, and the significance of praying to both qiblas.

Core Idea
The foremost Muslims are those who prayed to both qiblas.

The commentator clarifies that while the virtue of the foremost Muslims is established by explicit texts, their specific identity has been a subject of disagreement. This lesson explores the definition provided by Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, focusing on the criterion of having prayed to both qiblas, which highlights their early conversion and unique status.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the poetic line first, then the commentary.
The passage begins with a line from the matn, then a parenthetical explanation from the commentator.
2
Identify the key claims
The passage states two things: (1) their virtue is known by text, and (2) there is disagreement about who they are. Pause to consider why their identity might be debated.
3
Focus on the definition
According to Abu Musa al-Ash'ari and other great scholars, the foremost are those who prayed to both qiblas. Reflect on what praying to both qiblas implies about their time of conversion.
Praying to both qiblas means they were Muslims before the qibla changed from Jerusalem to Mecca.
4
Understand 'their virtue'
The commentator explains that 'their virtue' (fadluhum) means their superiority in abundance of reward. This shows that their merit is not merely chronological but also qualitative.
5
Connect to broader context
Consider why the two-qibla criterion is significant. It indicates they were among the earliest converts, having experienced both prayer directions.
This connects to the lesson's learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسَّابِقُونَ فَضلُهُم نصا عُرِفْ هذا وفي تَعْيِينِهِمْ قد اختُلِفْ (والسَّابِقُونَ الأَوَّلُونَ الذينَ صَلَّوْا إلى القبلتين كما قاله أبو موسى الأشعري وغيره من الأكابر (فَضْلُهُمْ)، أي: أرجحيتُهم في كثرة الثَّواب
And the foremost, their virtue is known by text; this, and regarding their determination, there is disagreement. (And the foremost, the first ones who prayed to the two qiblas, as Abu Musa al-Ash'ari and other great scholars said, (their virtue), meaning: their superiority in abundance of reward.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the 'foremost Muslims' (السابقون الأولون). The line of poetry states that their virtue is known by explicit text, but there is disagreement about who exactly they are. The commentator then explains that, according to Abu Musa al-Ash'ari and other eminent scholars, the foremost are those who prayed to both qiblas, i.e., they converted early enough to have prayed towards Jerusalem before the qibla was changed to Mecca. The commentator then clarifies that the word 'فضلهم' (their virtue) in the text means 'their superiority' (أرجحيتهم) in the abundance of reward. This indicates that their merit is not merely in being early, but in the greater reward they receive for their early faith and deeds.

Misconception: Some might think 'foremost' means simply the earliest converts in time, without a concrete criterion.
Correction: The passage specifies a concrete criterion: praying to both qiblas, which indicates early conversion before the qibla change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The foremost Muslims are defined not by their numbers or fame but by their consistent obedience through change. Right now, what is one area of your life where you have resisted change for the sake of Allah? Will you commit today to being among the foremost by embracing it?

The Scriptural Basis for the Companions' Excellence
Establishes Quranic basis for Companions' excellence and notes disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran establishes the virtue of the Companions and the scholarly disagreement over who are the السابقون الأولون.

Core Idea
The Quran itself is the basis for the Companions' excellence, not opinion.

This lesson begins the section on Prophethoods by establishing the scriptural basis for the virtue of the Companions. The author cites specific Quranic verses and then shows that scholars differed on who exactly are the السابقون الأولون. Understanding this sets the foundation for appreciating the rank of the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation to get the overall meaning.
The Arabic has some OCR issues, but the meaning is clear.
2
Identify the Quranic verses cited
Notice the two Quranic references: verse about السابقون الأولون and verse about spending before the conquest. They are the scriptural proof.
3
Note the scholarly opinions
The passage mentions two opinions on who are السابقون الأولون: al-Sha'bi says they are people of Bay'at al-Ridwan; Ibn Ka'b al-Qurazi says they are people of Badr.
This shows ikhtilaf (disagreement) among early scholars.
4
Understand the principle of ranking
The author explains that preference is by category (jins) not individuals, and some individuals may belong to multiple categories. The example of Uthman illustrates this.
This clarifies that virtues are compared by type, not by person.

قسم النبوات على غيرهم ممن لم يُشاركهم فيما ذكر، نَصَّا عُرِف أي: عُرف مِن نص القرآن، كقوله تعالى: ﴿وَالسَّبِقُونَ الْأَوَّلُونَ مِنَ الْمُهَاجِرِينَ وَالْأَنْصَارِ ... الآية. لَا يَسْتَوِى مِنكُر مَنْ أَنفَقَ مِن قَبْلِ الْفَتْحِ وَقَتَلَ ... وَفِي نَعْيِينِهِم يعني: الوصف المقتضي له المنطبق عليهم اختُلِفْ، أي: اختلف العلماء فيه، فقال الشَّعَبي: هم أهل بيعة الرضوان، وقال محمد بن كعب القرظي وجماعة: هم أهل بدر، والمُفضَّل في جميع هذه المراتب الجملة عن الجملة، لا الأفراد على الأفراد، وبعض أهل هذه المراتب ربَّما دخل في بعضها، وربَّما دخل في الجميع؛ فقد يكون سابقا خليفة بدريًّا أُحُديًّا رِضوانيًّا، كالمشايخ الأربعة: فإنَّ عثمان الله تعالى عنه بدري أجرًا لا حُضورًا، فمزيَّة البدري من حيث هو بدري لا تساويها مزيَّة الأُحدي من حيث هو أحدي مثلا، وإن أتحد محل المزيَّتين، وكذا الباقي.

'Not equal among you are those who spent before the conquest and fought...' Al-Sha'bi said: they are the people of the Pledge of Ridwan. Muhammad ibn Ka'b al-Qurazi and a group said: they are the people of Badr. Some people of these ranks may enter into some of them, and may enter into all: for one may be a forerunner, a caliph, a Badri, an Uhudi, a Ridwani, like the four elders. Indeed, Uthman, may Allah be pleased with him, is a Badri by reward, not by presence. So the virtue of being a Badri, insofar as it is a Badri, is not equal to the virtue of being an Uhudi insofar as it is an Uhudi, for example, even if the locus of the two virtues is united. And likewise the rest.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage begins by stating that the excellence of the Companions over others is known from the text of the Quran, citing the verse about the السابقون الأولون (the first forerunners) and the verse about those who spent before the conquest. Then it notes that there is disagreement among scholars about who exactly is meant by السابقون الأولون. It mentions the opinion of al-Sha'bi that they are the people of the Pledge of Ridwan, and the opinion of Muhammad ibn Ka'b al-Qurazi and a group that they are the people of Badr. The passage explains that the preference in these categories is by group, not by individuals, and that some individuals may belong to multiple categories. It gives the example of the four caliphs, specifically Uthman who is a Badri by reward but not by presence. It concludes that the virtue of being a Badri does not equal the virtue of being an Uhudi, even if the same person possesses both.

The Detailed Ranking of the Companions in Virtue
Enumerates the ranking of the Companions individually and by group, based on recognized order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the textual basis for the ranking of the Companions both individually and by group, as recognized in Sunni tradition.

Core Idea
The Companions' virtue ranking follows a divinely inspired order, not human opinion.

This passage from al-Barmawi's work distills the consensus on the ranking of the Companions. It shows that the order is derived from the textual arrangement (the Qur'an and hadith) and follows two clear methods: by specific individuals and by groups. Understanding this ranking helps affirm the proper respect for the Companions as per the Salaf.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the concise style and the two distinct categories of ranking.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage states that preference is either by individuals or by groups. Find these two sections and note how each is introduced.
3
List the individual order
The individual order is: Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman, then Ali. Memorize this sequence as the foundational ranking.
4
List the group order
The group order begins with the best (Abu Bakr), then Umar, then the four caliphs, then the six remaining of the ten, then the rest of Badr, Uhud, and finally the people of the Pledge of Ridwan. Trace each tier.
5
Reflect on the source
The passage attributes this ranking to Shams al-Barmawi and says it is 'known from the arrangement.' Consider what 'arrangement' refers to—likely the textual evidence from the Qur'an and hadith that establishes this order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد عُلم مِن النَّظم أنّ التَّفضيلَ: إمَّا باعتبار الأفراد، فأبو بكر ثمَّ عثمان، ثمَّ علي، وإما باعتبار الأصناف، فأفضلهم: الأفضل، ثُمَّ عمر، الخلفاء الأربعة، ثمَّ السِّتَّة الباقية من العشرة، ثمَّ بقيَّة البدرين، ثم بقيَّة أصحاب أحد، ثمّ بقيَّة أهل بيعة الرضوان بالحديبية، وهو في كلام الشَّمس البرماوي.
And it is known from the arrangement that the preference is either by individuals: so Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman, then Ali; or by groups: so the best of them is the best, then Umar, the four caliphs, then the six remaining of the ten, then the rest of the Badr participants, then the rest of the participants of Uhud, then the rest of the people of the Pledge of Ridwan at Hudaybiyyah. And it is in the words of Shams al-Barmawi.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage presents the detailed ranking of the Companions. It states that from the arrangement (of the Quranic verses or the prophetic data) we know that the preference is either by individuals or by groups. By individuals, the order is: Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman, then Ali. By groups, the best are: the best (i.e., Abu Bakr), then Umar, then the four caliphs (Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali), then the six remaining of the ten (the other six among the ten promised Paradise), then the rest of the Badr participants, then the rest of the participants of Uhud, then the rest of the people of the Pledge of Ridwan at Hudaybiyyah. The passage notes that this ranking is found in the words of Shams al-Barmawi.

Correction: The passage shows it is derived from the textual arrangement (al-nazm) and is a matter of knowledge, not preference.

Key Takeaway
The ranking of the Companions is not arbitrary; it follows a clear textual order starting with Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman, then Ali, and then groups by virtue.

?
This lesson shows a clear hierarchy of virtue among the Companions—and the highest is the most obedient and closest to the Prophet. Look at your own life: what is the basis on which you judge yourself or others as 'better' in faith? Is it sincerity, knowledge, action, or worldly status? Will you adjust your measure to match the revealed standard?
?
The Companions who were at Badr, Uhud, and the Pledge of Ridwan have a fixed rank that can never change. What about you? If you were to die today, what rank would you hold in Allah's sight? What specific action can you take today to improve your standing, even by one level, in the Hereafter?

The Preference Among the Prophet's Wives
Discusses the virtue of the Prophet's wives and the disagreement over which of Khadija and Aisha is superior.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that while Khadija and Aisha are the most virtuous of the Prophet's wives, there is a scholarly disagreement regarding which of them is superior, and that Ibn al-Imad favored Khadija.

Core Idea
The superiority between Khadija and Aisha is a matter of scholarly debate, not settled by consensus.

This lesson examines the question of preference among the Prophet's wives. While both Khadija and Aisha hold immense virtue, the author notes a difference of opinion over which is better. We'll explore the passage and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it begins with a general statement about the Prophet's wives and daughters, then narrows to the noble wives.
The passage is short but packed with doctrine.
2

The passage names Khadija and Aisha as the most virtuous. It explicitly says there is a difference of opinion (خلاف) on which is superior. Also note that Ibn al-Imad is mentioned as affirming Khadija's superiority.
This is a specific scholarly position, not the only one.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this disagreement exists and how it reflects the high status of both wives. The passage implies that the matter is not decisively settled, and both are extremely virtuous.

Subki's Ruling on the Best Women
Subki answers a question on preference of Fatimah, Khadijah, and Aisha, adding Maryam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Subki's specific ranking of Fatimah, Khadijah, Aisha, and Maryam, and how he harmonizes a hadith with the concept of prophethood.

Core Idea
Al-Subki ranks Fatimah first, then Maryam above Khadijah due to her prophethood.

This lesson presents the view of the renowned scholar Taj al-Din al-Subki on the preference among the Prophet's closest women. He was asked for his opinion and gave a detailed ranking, which includes Fatimah, Khadijah, Aisha, and also Maryam. The passage shows how he weighs a hadith and the issue of prophethood to arrive at his conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the order of names and the structure of the argument.
The passage has two main parts: the initial statement and the detailed answer.
2
Identify the initial ranking
Al-Subki begins by stating that Fatimah is better than Aisha. Then he gives a three-person ranking when asked for details.
Why does he start with Fatimah vs Aisha? The passage doesn't explain, but it sets the context.
3
Observe the addition of Maryam
Al-Subki adds that Maryam is better than Khadijah, based on a hadith and the difference of opinion on her prophethood.
This shows he is not merely repeating a hadith but reasoning about it.
4
Reflect on the harmony
Consider how al-Subki's ranking aligns with the hadith while still maintaining Fatimah's superiority over Aisha.
The common misunderstanding is that the rankings contradict; see how they are harmonized.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم النبوات وفاطمة (١) ، فتكون أفضل من عائشة . ولمَّا سُئل السُّبكي (۲) عن ذلك (۳) قال : الَّذِي نختارهُ ونَدِينُ اللَّهَ بِه أَنَّ فاطمة بنت سيدنا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم أفضل ، ثمَّ أَمُّها خديجة ، ثمَّ عائشة . واختار السبكي : أنَّ مريمَ أفضلُ مِن خديجة (٤) ، لقوله : «خير نساء العالَمِينَ مريم بنت عمران ثمَّ خديجة بنت خويلد، ثمَّ فاطمة بنت محمد، ثم آسية امرأة فرعون » ( ه ) ، وللاختلاف في نبوّتها .
Section of prophethoods. And Fatimah (1), so she is better than Aisha. And when al-Subki (2) was asked about that (3), he said: That which we choose and by which we worship Allah is that Fatimah the daughter of our master Muhammad (peace be upon him) is best, then her mother Khadijah, then Aisha. And al-Subki chose that Maryam is better than Khadijah (4), because of his statement: 'The best of the women of the worlds are Maryam bint Imran, then Khadijah bint Khuwaylid, then Fatimah bint Muhammad, then Asiyah the wife of Pharaoh' (5), and because of the difference concerning her prophethood.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage presents al-Subki's view on the preference among the Prophet's womenfolk. It begins by stating that Fatimah is better than Aisha. Then, when asked for a detailed ruling, al-Subki specifies the order: Fatimah, then her mother Khadijah, then Aisha. He then adds that Maryam is better than Khadijah, citing a hadith that lists the best women of the worlds: Maryam, Khadijah, Fatimah, and Asiyah. The passage notes that this additional ruling is due to the difference of opinion regarding Maryam's prophethood, which elevates her status.

Shaykh al-Islam's View on the Preference of the Prophet's Family
Shaykh al-Islam explains that preference is relative to different qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Shaykh al-Islam Zakariyya al-Ansari taught that preference among the Prophet's family and other noble women is not absolute but relative to different aspects, and that the basis of preference (circumstances vs reward) affects whether one can rank them.

Core Idea
Preference among the Prophet's family is not absolute; each excels in a different quality.

This passage presents Shaykh al-Islam's nuanced view on a sensitive topic. Instead of declaring one woman absolutely superior, he shows that each has a distinct excellence—knowledge, precedence, kinship, or divine mention. He also explains that if we consider reward, we should withhold ranking. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Shaykh al-Islam uses a very measured tone.
2
Identify the women and their virtues
The passage lists several women: Aisha, Khadijah, Fatimah, Maryam, and Asiyah. For each, note the specific quality that makes her superior in that aspect.
This is the core of the argument: preference is relative.
3
Understand the condition
Shaykh al-Islam adds a condition: if preference is based on circumstances and beautiful qualities, his view stands; if based on reward, withholding judgment is safer. Understand the difference.
This shows his scholarly caution.
4
Reflect on the resolution
Consider how this approach resolves conflicting reports about their preference. Each report can be interpreted according to a specific virtue.
This is a key principle for harmonizing texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال شيخُ الإسلام في شرح البخاري: الذي أختارُه الآنَ أَنَّ الأفضلية محمولة على أحوال ، فعائشةُ أفضلهنَّ مِن حيثُ العِلم، وخديجة من حيثُ تَقدُّمُها وإعانتها له في المهمات ، وفاطمة من حيثُ القرابة ، ومريم من حيثُ الاختلافُ في نُبُوَّتها وذكرها في القرآن مع الأنبياء، وآسية امرأة فرعون من هذه الحيثية ، لكن لم تُذكر مع الأنبياء، وعلى ذلك تُنَزَّلُ الأخبار الواردة في أفضليَّتِهنَّ . اهـ . وهذا جيّد إن قلنا: إن التفضيل بالأحوال وكثرة الخصال الجميلة ، وأما إن قلنا : إنَّه باعتبار كثرةِ الثَّواب، فالأقرب الوقف، كما هو قول الأشعري له . وفي كلام البرهان الحلبي: أنَّ زينب بنت جحش تلي عائشة رضي الله تعالى عنهما، ولم يقف أستاذنا على نص في باقيهنَّ، ولا مفاضلة في رضوان.
And Shaykh al-Islam said in his commentary on al-Bukhari: That which I now choose is that preference is based on circumstances. So Aisha is the best of them with respect to knowledge, Khadijah with respect to her precedence and assistance to him in important matters, Fatimah with respect to kinship, Maryam with respect to the difference concerning her prophethood and her mention in the Quran with the prophets, and Asiyah the wife of Pharaoh is from this perspective, but she is not mentioned with the prophets. And upon that the reports concerning their preference are to be interpreted. End. And this is good if we say that preference is by circumstances and abundance of beautiful qualities. But if we say that it is by abundance of reward, then the closest is to withhold, as is the statement of al-Ash'ari. And in the speech of al-Burhan al-Halabi: that Zaynab bint Jahsh follows Aisha (may Allah be pleased with both of them), and our teacher did not find a text regarding the rest of them, nor a preference concerning divine pleasure.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage presents Shaykh al-Islam Zakariyya al-Ansari's nuanced view on the preference among the Prophet's family and other noble women. He states that preference is not absolute but depends on specific aspects (ahwal). He then lists the distinct excellence of each: Aisha excels in knowledge, Khadijah in early support and assistance, Fatimah in kinship, Maryam due to the debate about her prophethood and her mention in the Quran alongside prophets, and Asiyah similarly but without being mentioned with prophets. He says that traditions about their preference should be interpreted accordingly. He then adds that if preference is based on circumstances and good qualities, this view stands; but if based on reward, withholding judgment is safer, following al-Ash'ari. Finally, he reports al-Burhan al-Halabi's view that Zaynab bint Jahsh comes after Aisha, but no text is available for the others or for ranking in divine pleasure.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Shaykh al-Islam ranks Aisha as absolutely best over all others.
Correction: He shows each has a distinct excellence: Aisha in knowledge, Khadijah in early support, etc. Preference is relative.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Definition and Conditions of Karama
Sanusi defines karama and lists its distinguishing conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of karama and how it is distinguished from other types of extraordinary events.

Core Idea
Karama is a wonder from a righteous follower, not a prophet.

This passage from Sanusi's Jawharat al-Tawhid establishes a precise definition of karama, the wonder granted to saints. By laying out conditions and exclusions, Sanusi ensures we do not confuse karama with miracles of prophets, wonders before prophethood, or deceptive phenomena. Let's examine the definition step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and conditions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the full definition and the list of conditions.
Notice that the definition begins with 'أمر خارق للعادة'.
2
Identify the exclusions
The passage uses phrases like 'فدخل في قولنا' and 'يخرج' to include and exclude types. Identify what each condition excludes.
There are four main exclusions: mu'jiza, irhas, ma'unah, ihanah, istidraj, sihr.
3
Analyze the example of ihanah
The author gives an example: Musaylimah's spitting into the well. Reflect on why this is ihanah, not karama.
This helps understand the condition of righteousness.
4
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to check if you can distinguish karama from other wonders.
The question is based directly on the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف الكرامة وحُدودُها في خَرْقِ العَادات والكرامة: أمر خارق للعادة غير مقرون بدعوى النُّبوَّة، ولا هو مقدمة لها، يظهر على يد عبدٍ ظاهرِ الصَّلاحِ، مُلتزم لمتابعة نبي كُلف بشريعته، الاعتقاد، والعمل الصالح، عَلِمَ بها أو لم يعلم. مصحوب بصحيح فدخل في قولنا «أمرٌ خارق» - جنسُ الخَوارِق، وخرج بغير مقرون بدعوى «النبوة المعجزة وينفي مقدّمتها الإرهاص، وبظهور الصَّلاح ما يُسمَّى مَعونةً ممَّا يظهر على يد بعض العوام، وبالتزام متابعة نبيَّ ما يسمَّى إهانةً، كالخَوارِق المؤكَّدة لكذب الكاذبين، كبصق مسيلمة في البئر، وبالمصحوبيَّة بصحيح الاعتقاد الاستدراج، كما خرج السحر من جهات عِدة.
Definition of karama and its limits in breaking customs. Karama: a matter breaking custom, not accompanied by a claim of prophethood, nor is it a prelude to it, appearing at the hand of a servant whose righteousness is apparent, committed to following a prophet charged with his law, [with] correct belief and righteous action, whether he knows it or not. Accompanied by correctness. So enters into our saying 'a matter breaking custom' the genus of wonders, and is excluded by 'not accompanied by a claim of prophethood' the miracle and negates its prelude, irhas; and by 'appearance of righteousness' what is called ma'unah from what appears at the hand of some common people; and by 'commitment to following a prophet' what is called ihanah, like the wonders confirming the lie of liars, like Musaylimah's spitting into the well; and by 'accompanied by correct belief' istidraj, as sorcery is excluded from several aspects.
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage is a formal definition of karama with its limiting conditions. It begins with the genus: 'a matter breaking custom'. Then it adds conditions to distinguish karama from other wonders. The first condition: not accompanied by a claim of prophethood—this excludes miracles of prophets (mu'jizat). Second: not a prelude to prophethood—this excludes irhas (wonders before prophethood). Third: appears on a righteous servant committed to following a prophet—this excludes ma'unah (which may appear on non-committed people) and ihanah (wonders that support liars, e.g., Musaylimah). Fourth: accompanied by correct belief and righteous action—this excludes istidraj (gradual deception) and sihr (sorcery). The passage explains each exclusion in sequence.

Key Terms
كرامة
معجزة
إرهاص
إهانة
استدراج
سحر
مَعونة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Karama and mu'jiza are the same kind of wonder.
Correction: Karama is not accompanied by a claim of prophethood, while mu'jiza is specifically a miracle affirming prophethood.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is excluded from karama by the condition 'not accompanied by a claim of prophethood'?
A) Istidraj
B) Mu'jiza
C) Ihanah
D) Ma'unah

Key Takeaway
Karama is a wonder from a righteous follower of a prophet, distinguished by specific conditions that prevent confusion with other wonders.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever attributed a personal success or unusual event to your own piety? How would you distinguish between a genuine karama and a test of istidraj in your own life? What measure can you adopt to ensure your faith and actions are sincere?
?
If karama is described as appearing on a servant 'whose righteousness is apparent and committed to following a prophet', what are you actively doing to move beyond being a 'common person' (عوام) to someone who could be worthy of such a wonder? What step can you take this week to deepen your commitment to following the Prophet ﷺ in both belief and action?
?
The source teaches that wonders can be from Allah to honor a believer or to deceive a wrongdoer. How does this make you reflect on your own intentions and the authenticity of your religious practice? Are you seeking Allah's pleasure or merely outward signs of 'success'? What will you change today to prioritize correct belief over show?

Definition and Benefit of Duʿāʾ
The author defines duʿāʾ and affirms its benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ahl al-Sunna definition of duʿāʾ and its benefit for both the living and the dead, based on the Quranic promise.

Core Idea
Duʿāʾ is raising needs to Allah, and it truly benefits according to the Quran.

In this passage, the commentator defines the term duʿāʾ and affirms its practical benefit. Duʿāʾ is not merely a ritual; it is a real means of effect, supported by a Quranic promise. The author also responds to the Muʿtazila who deny its benefit. Let's explore the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Start by reading the Arabic and the literal translation together to get a sense of the author's statement.
Notice the poetic line and its explanation.
2
Identify the definition of duʿāʾ
The author defines duʿāʾ as raising needs to the Elevator of degrees. Reflect on what this means about the nature of supplication.
This definition emphasizes that duʿāʾ is a directed request to Allah.
3
Understand the benefit claimed
The text states that duʿāʾ benefits the living and the dead, based on a Quranic promise. Consider how this differs from the Mu'tazila position who say it does not benefit.
The benefit is for both living and dead, which is a distinctive Sunni view.
4
Reflect on the refutation
The author alludes to refuting the Mu'tazila who deny duʿāʾ's benefit. Note how the text uses the Quran as evidence.
This shows the importance of Quranic authority in theological debates.

Commentary
This passage defines duʿāʾ and states its benefit. The author begins with a heading: 'Definition of duʿāʾ and the clarification that duʿāʾ benefits the living and the dead according to Ahl al-Sunna.' Then he quotes the poetic line: 'And according to us, duʿāʾ is profitable, as from the Quran a promise is heard.' Then he explains that this line is a refutation of the Muʿtazila who claim that duʿāʾ does not benefit. He paraphrases the meaning: duʿāʾ is raising needs to the Elevator of degrees (i.e., Allah). Thus, duʿāʾ is a request to Allah, and it is beneficial based on the Quranic promise that Allah answers prayers.

Du'a Benefits the Living and Dead: A Sunni Doctrine
Definition of du'a, its benefit, and types of qada'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni doctrine that du'a benefits both the living and the dead, and how it relates to the two types of divine decree (qada').

Core Idea
Du'a is effective; it can change conditional decrees and earn reward even for irrevocable ones.

The author begins by affirming a core Sunni belief: supplication (du'a) is beneficial for both the living and the dead, countering the Mu'tazila who deny its effect. He then defines du'a, explains what benefit means, and presents a nuanced understanding of how du'a interacts with divine decree by distinguishing between two types of qada'. This lesson unpacks that argument and its evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the author's style: he defines, refutes, and distinguishes.
The passage includes a hadith and a classification of qada'.
2
Understand the definition and scope of du'a
Focus on how the author defines du'a ('raising needs to the Raiser of degrees') and the claim that it benefits even if from a disbeliever. Why does he mention that?
This refutes the Mu'tazila who say du'a has no benefit.
3
Analyze the two types of qada'
The passage divides qada' into mubram (irrevocable) and mu'allaq (conditional). Identify how du'a affects each type according to Ahl al-Sunna.
For mu'allaq, du'a can change the outcome; for mubram, it may earn reward or grace.
4
Connect to the overall lesson
Reflect on why this distinction matters: it preserves both divine sovereignty and the efficacy of du'a. How does the hadith of the oppressed support the point?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريفُ الدُّعاءِ وبيان أنَّ الدعاء ينفعُ الأحياء والأموات عند أهل السُّنَّة. وَعِنْدَنا أَنَّ الدُّعاءَ يَنْفَعُ كما مِنَ الْقُرْآنِ وَعْدًا يُسْمَعُ. وأشار إلى رَدّ قول المُعتزلة أيضاً إنَّ الدُّعَاءَ لا ينفعُ (٢)، بقوله: (وَعِنْدَنَا) أهل السُّنَّة (أَنَّ الدُّعَاء) (۳) وهو رفع الحاجات (٤) إلى رافع الدرجات (يَنْفَعُ) ممَّا نَزَلَ وممَّا لم ينزل فينفعُ الأحياء والأموات ويَضرُّهم، والنفع: الخير، وهو ما يتوصل به الإنسان إلى مطلوبه. فالدُّعَاء يُوصِل إلى المطلوب ولو صَدَر من كافر (۱)؛ لحديث أنس: دعوة المظلومِ مُستجابةٌ، وإِنْ كانَ كافرًا (۲). والقضاء على قِسْمَيْن: مُبْرَمٌ، ومُعَلَّقٌ (۳). فالمُعلَّق: لا استحالة (٤) في رفع ما عُلِّق رفعه منه على الدُّعاء، ولا في نُزول ما عُلِّق نزوله منه على الدُّعاء. وأمَّا المُبْرَمُ: فالدُّعَاءُ وإنْ لم يرفعه؛ لكنْ ربَّما أَثَابَ الله العبد على دُعائه برفعه، أو أَنْزَلَ بالدَّاعِي لُطفَه فيه، والمُدَّعى (٥) تَرتُبُ نفع للداعي أو لغيره بلسان التضرع.
Definition of supplication (du'a) and clarification that supplication benefits the living and the dead according to the People of the Sunna. And with us (the People of the Sunna) that supplication benefits, as a promise heard from the Qur'an. And he [the author] pointed to refuting the statement of the Mu'tazila also that supplication does not benefit, by his saying: 'And with us,' the People of the Sunna, 'that supplication' – and it is the raising of needs to the Raiser of degrees – 'benefits' from what has descended and from what has not descended, so it benefits the living and the dead and harms them. And benefit (al-naf') is good (al-khayr), and it is that by which a person attains his sought object. So supplication conveys to the sought object even if it issues from a disbeliever, due to the hadith of Anas: 'The supplication of the oppressed is answered, even if he is a disbeliever.' And the decree (al-qada') is of two types: irrevocable (mubram) and conditional (mu'allaq). As for the conditional, there is no impossibility in removing that whose removal has been made dependent on supplication from it, nor in the descent of that whose descent has been made dependent on supplication from it. As for the irrevocable, supplication, even if it does not remove it, perhaps God may reward the servant for his supplication by removing it, or may send down His grace to the supplicant in it, and what is claimed [by the People of the Sunna] is the occurrence of benefit to the supplicant or to others with the tongue of humility.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage defines du'a (supplication) and argues for its benefit, countering the Mu'tazila who deny it. The author first states that according to Ahl al-Sunna, du'a benefits both the living and the dead, citing the Qur'anic promise. He then explains that du'a is the act of raising needs to God, and benefit is defined as the good by which one attains his goal. The passage emphasizes that du'a achieves its objective even if made by a disbeliever, supported by a hadith. It then distinguishes two types of qada' (divine decree): mubram (irrevocable) and mu'allaq (conditional). For mu'allaq, du'a can affect it by removing or bringing what is conditioned upon it. For mubram, while du'a does not change it, God may reward the supplicant by removing it or by bestowing His grace. The claimed position is that du'a results in benefit for the supplicant or others, offered with humility.

نفع
قضاء
مبرم
معلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Du'a has no effect on qada' because everything is already decreed and unchangeable.
Correction: The passage clarifies that qada' includes a mu'allaq type that is conditional upon du'a, so du'a can affect it. For mubram, du'a still yields reward or divine grace.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two types of qada' (divine decree) and how does du'a affect each?

B) Mubram and mu'allaq; du'a can affect mu'allaq but not mubram, though God may reward.
C) Mubram and mu'allaq; du'a has no effect on either.
D) Mubram and mu'allaq; du'a cancels both types.

Key Takeaway
Du'a is effective: it can alter conditional decrees and earn divine reward even for irrevocable ones, affirming the Sunni balance between divine sovereignty and human recourse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Definition of Du'a, Its Benefit, and Two Types of Qada
Sanusi defines du'a, states its benefit, and classifies qada into mubram and mu'allaq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of du'a, its universal benefit, and the distinction between absolute and conditional divine decrees.

Core Idea
Supplication benefits everyone, even disbelievers, and can change conditional decrees.

This passage from Sanusi's text defines the nature and benefit of supplication (du'a) and introduces a key distinction in divine decree. Du'a is not just a ritual; it is a cause that brings about benefit, even for the dead or disbelievers. Understanding the two types of qada—absolute (mubram) and conditional (mu'allaq)—helps clarify how supplication interacts with God's foreknowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage

Understand the benefit of du'a

Conditional decree can be lifted; absolute decree may not be removed but brings reward.

The common misunderstanding is that du'a only benefits the supplicant. How does this passage show otherwise?
Du'a benefits others as well, as it is a cause for God's kindness.

( يَنْفَعُ) ممَّا نَزَلَ وممَّا لم ينزل فينفعُ الأحياء والأموات ويَضرُّهم، والنفع: الخير ، وهو ما يتوصل به الإنسان إلى مطلوبه. فالدُّعَاء يُوصِل إلى المطلوب ولو صَدَر من كافر (۱) ؛ لحديث أنس : دعوة المظلومِ مُستجابةٌ ، وإِنْ كانَ كافرًا ) (۲) . والقضاء على قِسْمَيْن : مُبْرَمٌ ، ومُعَلَّقٌ (۳) . فالمُعلَّق: لا استحالة (٤) في رفع ما عُلِّق رفعه منه على الدُّعاء، ولا في نُزول ما عُلِّق نزوله منه على الدُّعاء. وأمَّا المُبْرَمُ: فالدُّعَاءُ وإنْ لم يرفعه ؛ لكنْ ربَّما أَثَابَ الله العبد على دُعائه برفعه ، أو أَنْزَلَ بالدَّاعِي لُطفَه فيه ، والمُدَّعى(٥) تَرتُبُ نفع للداعي أو لغيره - بلسان التضرع . وقال السعد: إنه الطلب على سبيل التضرع الشنواني .
It benefits from what has been sent down and from what has not been sent down, so it benefits the living and the dead and harms them. Benefit: goodness, and it is that by which a person attains his object. So supplication (al-du'ā) leads to the object, even if it comes from a disbeliever (1); due to the hadith of Anas: 'The supplication of the oppressed is answered, even if he is a disbeliever' (2). And the decree (al-qaḍā) is of two divisions: absolute (mubram) and conditional (mu'allaq) (3). The conditional: there is no impossibility (4) in removing what was conditioned upon its removal from supplication, nor in the descent of what was conditioned upon its descent from supplication. As for the absolute: supplication, even if it does not remove it, but perhaps God rewards the servant for his supplication by removing it, or He brings down upon the supplicant His kindness in it, and it is claimed (5) that benefit accrues to the supplicant or to others - with the tongue of humility. And al-Sa'd said: 'It is a request in the manner of humility' al-Shanwānī.

Commentary
This passage defines the benefit of supplication (du'a) and categorizes the divine decree into two types: absolute (mubram) and conditional (mu'allaq). It begins by stating that supplication benefits both the living and the dead, and defines benefit as that which leads a person to their goal. It then asserts that supplication leads to the desired outcome even if performed by a disbeliever, citing a hadith. The passage then divides divine decree into two: mubram (absolute) and mu'allaq (conditional). For the conditional, there is no impossibility in lifting or sending down what is conditioned upon supplication. For the absolute, supplication may not lift it, but God may reward the servant by lifting it or bestow kindness. The result is benefit for the supplicant or others. It ends with a definition of du'a as a request in humility.

Quranic Proof for the Benefit of Du'a and the Varieties of Response
The author provides Quranic evidence for du'a's benefit and explains the response types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Quranic basis for the benefit of supplication and the three ways in which God may respond to a believer's call.

Core Idea
God's response to du'a is guaranteed, but not always immediate or in the exact form requested.

This lesson establishes the theological foundation for the benefit of supplication (du'a) in Islam. We will examine the Quranic verses that promise response, and then explore how qualified scholars have understood the nature of divine response — which can be immediate, delayed, or replaced with something better. The passage removes any notion that du'a is futile or that lack of immediate response indicates rejection.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Quranic proof for du'a's benefit
Read the two verses cited (Ghafir 40:60 and Baqarah 2:186) and note how they form the basis of the promise.
2
Recognize the qualification of these verses
The third verse (An'am 6:41) qualifies that response is contingent on God's will. This removes the misconception that du'a is automatically accepted in the exact form.
3
Learn the three categories of divine response
The hadith of Musa and the scholars' explanation outline three possibilities: immediate grant, delayed grant, or granting something else (either averting harm or storing reward).
4
Reflect on the wisdom behind delayed or substituted responses
Consider why a response might be delayed or given in a different form: it may be more beneficial for the supplicant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف الدُّعاءِ وبيان أنَّ الدعاء ينفعُ الأحياء والأموات على دعائه عاجلا أو آجلا - يُخْرِجُه عن العَبثِيَّة . به وجَزْمُنَا الاعتقادَ بِنَفْعِ الدُّعَاءِ كَمَا مِنَ القُرْآنِ وَعْدًا) أي: لأنَّ اللَّهَ وعَدَ في القُرآن حالَ كونِ ذلكَ الموعودِ به (يُسْمَعُ) مِن تلاوته، قال تعالى: وَقَالَ رَبُّكُمُ ادْعُونِي أَسْتَجِبْ لَكُمْ﴾ [غافر: ٦٠] ، وَإِذَا سَأَلَكَ عِبَادِي عَنِي فَإِنِّي قَرِيبٌ أُجِيبُ دَعْوَةَ الدَّاعِ إِذَا دَعَانِ﴾ [البقرة: ١٨٦] . عنهم وإطلاق هاتين الآيتين يُقيَّدُه (۱) قوله تعالى: ﴿فَيَكْشِفُ مَا تَدْعُونَ إِلَيْهِ إِن شَاءَ ﴾ [الأنعام: ٤١] ، فالمَرَادُ (۲) : الإجابة المُصرَّحُ بها في حديث مُناجاة موسى «وَإِنْ دعوني أستجب لهم ، فإما أن يَروه عاجلا ، وإِمَّا أَنْ أَصرِفَ سُوءًا ، وإِمَّا أنْ أدَّخِره لهم في الآخرة» (۳) وفي كلام بعضهم: أنّ الإجابة تتنوع ؛ فتارةً يقع المطلوب بعينه على الفور، وتارةً يقعُ ولكن يتأخَّر لحكمة فيه، وتارةً تَقَعُ الإجابةُ بغير عين المطلوب؛ حيثُ لا يكون في المطلوب مَصلحةٌ ناجزةٌ وفي الواقع مصلحة ناجزة ، أو أصلح منها.
Definition of supplication and clarification that supplication benefits the living and the dead upon his supplication, sooner or later - it removes it from futility. And our affirmation of belief in the benefit of supplication is as from the Qur'an as a promise, i.e., because God promised in the Qur'an, that promised thing being (heard) from its recitation. He said: 'And your Lord says: Call upon Me, I will respond to you' [Ghafir: 60], and 'And when My servants ask you about Me, then indeed I am near; I respond to the invocation of the supplicant when he calls upon Me' [Al-Baqarah: 186]. The generality of these two verses is qualified (1) by His saying: 'Then He will uncover that for which you prayed to Him if He wills' [Al-An'am: 41]. So what is meant (2) is the response explicitly stated in the hadith of Musa's conversation: 'And if they call upon Me, I will respond to them; either they will see it soon, or I will turn away evil, or I will store it for them in the Hereafter' (3). And in the speech of some: that the response varies; sometimes the very object sought occurs immediately, sometimes it occurs but is delayed for a wisdom in it, and sometimes the response occurs with other than the very object sought, where there is no immediate benefit in the sought object and there is an immediate benefit in what is realized, or one more beneficial.
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage provides the Quranic proof for the benefit of supplication and explains the nature of divine response. It defines supplication and states that it benefits the living and dead, removing futility. It affirms belief in supplication's benefit based on Quranic promises, citing two verses (Ghafir 40:60 and Al-Baqarah 2:186). It then addresses an implicit question: why do some supplications seem unanswered? The author explains that these verses are qualified by another verse (Al-An'am 6:41) indicating that response is conditional on God's will. The response is thus not automatic; it can be immediate, delayed, or replaced with something better, as reported in the hadith of Musa's conversation. The passage ends by enumerating three ways response can occur: immediate grant of the exact request, delayed grant, or granting something else (either a different benefit in this world or postponed reward in the Hereafter).

إجابة
وعد

صرف سوء
ادخار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God must immediately grant exactly what is asked, otherwise the supplication is rejected.
Correction: The passage clarifies that response may be delayed or replaced with something more beneficial, based on divine wisdom.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, how are the two Quranic verses promising response to supplication qualified?
A) They are abrogated by later verses.
B) They are qualified by the verse that conditions response on God's will.
C) They apply only to believers.
D) They are unconditional promises.

Key Takeaway
God's promise to respond to supplication is true, but the response may take different forms — immediate, delayed, or replaced with something better — according to His wisdom.

By the end of this lesson, you will understand that du'a benefits both the living and the dead, and that Allah's response takes one of three wise forms.

Du'a is never futile; Allah responds in one of three wise ways.

This passage begins by defining supplication and asserting its benefit, supported by Qur'anic promises and a prophetic hadith. It clarifies that the response to du'a is not always immediate or identical to the request, but always beneficial.

2

Find the two verses promising response and the verse that restricts absoluteness. Then locate the hadith of Musa that outlines the three forms of response.
3
Understand the three forms of response
List the three forms: immediate granting, averting evil, or storing reward. Also note the additional explanation about immediate, delayed, or replaced with better.
This is the core benefit of du'a.
